      <p><b>Bosnia and Herzegovina</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup> abbreviated <b>BiH</b> or <b>B&amp;H</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[b]</a></sup> sometimes called <b>BosniaâHerzegovina</b> and <a href="/wiki/Pars_pro_toto#Geography" title="Pars pro toto">often known informally as</a> <b>Bosnia</b>, is a country in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast Europe</a>, located within the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> is the capital and largest city. </p>
      <p>Bosnia and Herzegovina is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> to the southeast, and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> to the north and southwest. It is not entirely <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked</a>; to the south it has a narrow coast on the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a>, which is about 20 kilometres (12 miles) long and surrounds the town of <a href="/wiki/Neum" title="Neum">Neum</a>. The inland <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_(region)" title="Bosnia (region)">Bosnia</a> region has a moderate <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climate</a>, with hot summers and cold, snowy winters. In the central and eastern interior of the country the geography is mountainous, in the northwest moderately hilly, and in the northeast predominantly flatland. The smaller southern region, <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina" title="Herzegovina">Herzegovina</a>, has a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a> and mostly mountainous topography. </p>
      <p>Bosnia and Herzegovina has been settled since at least the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> but permanent human settlement traces back to the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> age, during which time it was inhabited by cultures such as <a href="/wiki/Butmir_culture" title="Butmir culture">Butmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kakanj_culture" title="Kakanj culture">Kakanj</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vu%C4%8Dedol_culture" title="VuÄedol culture">VuÄedol</a>. After the arrival of the first <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-Europeans</a>, it was populated by several <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> civilizations. Culturally, politically, and socially, the country has a rich but complex history, having been first settled by the <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South Slavic</a> peoples that populate the area today from the 6th through to the 9th centuries. In the 12th century the <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Banate of Bosnia</a> was established, which evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bosnia" title="Kingdom of Bosnia">Kingdom of Bosnia</a> in the 14th century, after which it was annexed into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, under whose rule it remained from the mid-15th to the late 19th centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Islam in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Islam</a> to the region, and altered much of the cultural and social outlook of the country. This was followed by <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_rule_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Austro-Hungarian rule in Bosnia and Herzegovina">annexation into the Austro-Hungarian Monarchy</a>, which lasted up until World War I. In the interwar period, Bosnia and Herzegovina was part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">Kingdom of Yugoslavia</a> and after World War II, it was granted full republic status in the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>. Following the dissolution of Yugoslavia, the republic <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia#Independence_of_the_Republic_of_Macedonia_and_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Breakup of Yugoslavia">proclaimed independence</a> in 1992, which was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>, lasting until late 1995 and culminating with the <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Agreement" title="Dayton Agreement">Dayton Agreement</a>. </p>
      <p>The country is home to three main ethnic groups or, officially, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Ethnic groups in Bosnia and Herzegovina">constituent peoples</a>, as specified in the constitution. <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniaks</a> are the largest group of the three, with <a href="/wiki/Serbs_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Serbs of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Serbs</a> second, and <a href="/wiki/Croats_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Croats of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Croats</a> third. A native of Bosnia and Herzegovina, regardless of ethnicity, is usually identified in English as a <a href="/wiki/Bosnians" title="Bosnians">Bosnian</a>. Minorities, defined <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Ethnic groups in Bosnia and Herzegovina">under the constitutional nomenclature "Others"</a>, include <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="History of the Jews in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romani_people_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Romani people in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Roma</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_minorities_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="National minorities in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Ukrainians, and Turks</a>. Bosnia and Herzegovina has a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> legislature and a three-member Presidency composed of a member of each major ethnic group. However, the central government's power is highly limited, as the country is largely decentralized and comprises two autonomous entities: the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republika_Srpska" title="Republika Srpska">Republika Srpska</a>, with a third unit, the <a href="/wiki/Br%C4%8Dko_District" title="BrÄko District">BrÄko District</a>, governed under local government. The Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina consists of 10 <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_the_Federation_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Cantons of the Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina">cantons</a>. </p>
      <p>Bosnia and Herzegovina is a <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing country</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">ranks 73rd in human development</a>. Its economy is dominated by the industry and agriculture sectors, followed by the tourism and service sectors, the former of which has seen a significant rise in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> The country has a social security and universal healthcare system, and primary- and secondary-level education is tuition-free. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">PfP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_European_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="Central European Free Trade Agreement">Central European Free Trade Agreement</a>, and a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_the_Mediterranean" title="Union for the Mediterranean">Union for the Mediterranean</a> upon its establishment in July 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> The country is an <a href="/wiki/Accession_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_to_the_European_Union" title="Accession of Bosnia and Herzegovina to the European Union">applicant for membership to the European Union</a> and has been a candidate for <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> membership since April 2010, when it received a <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO#Membership_Action_Plan" title="Enlargement of NATO">Membership Action Plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <p>The first preserved widely acknowledged mention of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_(region)" title="Bosnia (region)">Bosnia</a> is in <i><a href="/wiki/De_Administrando_Imperio" title="De Administrando Imperio">De Administrando Imperio</a></i>, a politico-geographical handbook written by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">Byzantine emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a> in the mid-10th century (between 948 and 952) describing the "small land" (ÏÏÏÎ¯Î¿Î½ in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>) of "Bosona" (ÎÎ¿ÏÏÎ½Î±), where the Serbs dwell.<sup id="cite_ref-Moravcsik-153_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moravcsik-153-18">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The name is believed to have derived from the <a href="/wiki/Hydronym" title="Hydronym">hydronym</a> of the river <a href="/wiki/Bosna_(river)" title="Bosna (river)">Bosna</a> coursing through the Bosnian heartland. According to <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philologist</a> Anton Mayer the name <i>Bosna</i> could derive from <a href="/wiki/Illyrian_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Illyrian languages">Illyrian</a> *"Bass-an-as"), which would derive from the Proto-Indo-European root "bos" or "bogh"âmeaning "the running water".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> According to English medievalist <a href="/wiki/William_Miller_(historian)" title="William Miller (historian)">William Miller</a> the Slavic settlers in Bosnia "adapted the Latin designation [...] Basante, to their own idiom by calling the stream Bosna and themselves <i>Bosniaks</i> [...]".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The name <i>Herzegovina</i> ("herzog's [land]", from German word for "duke")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> originates from Bosnian magnate <a href="/wiki/Stjepan_Vuk%C4%8Di%C4%87_Kosa%C4%8Da" title="Stjepan VukÄiÄ KosaÄa">Stjepan VukÄiÄ KosaÄa</a>'s title, "Herceg (Herzog) of Hum and the Coast" (1448).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1994578_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1994578-21">[19]</a></sup> Hum, formerly <a href="/wiki/Zachlumia" title="Zachlumia">Zachlumia</a>, was an early medieval principality that was conquered by the Bosnian Banate in the first half of the 14th century. The region was administered by the Ottomans as the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Herzegovina" title="Sanjak of Herzegovina">Sanjak of Herzegovina</a> (<i>Hersek</i>) within the <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_Eyalet" title="Bosnia Eyalet">Bosnia Eyalet</a> up until the formation of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina_Eyalet" title="Herzegovina Eyalet">Herzegovina Eyalet</a> in the 1830s, which remerged in the 1850s, after which the entity became commonly known as <i>Bosnia and Herzegovina</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>On initial proclamation of independence in 1992, the country's official name was the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> but following the 1995 <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Agreement" title="Dayton Agreement">Dayton Agreement</a> and the new constitution that accompanied it the official name was changed to Bosnia and Herzegovina.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_antiquity">Prehistory and antiquity</span></h3> 
      <p>Bosnia has been inhabited by humans since at least the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, as one of the oldest cave paintings was found in <a href="/wiki/Badanj_Cave" title="Badanj Cave">Badanj cave</a>. Major Neolithic cultures such as the <a href="/wiki/Butmir_culture" title="Butmir culture">Butmir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kakanj_culture" title="Kakanj culture">Kakanj</a> were present along the river <a href="/wiki/Bosna_(river)" title="Bosna (river)">Bosna</a> dated from c. 6230 BC â c. 4900 BC. </p>
      <p>The bronze culture of the <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrians</a>, an ethnic group with a distinct culture and art form, started to organize itself in today's <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, Bosnia and Herzegovina, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="ref_status"><a href="#endnote_status">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>. </p>
      <p>From 8th century BC, Illyrian tribes evolved into kingdoms. The earliest recorded kingdom in <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a> (a region in the western part of the Balkan Peninsula inhabited by the Illyrians, as recorded in classical antiquity) was the Enchele in the 8th century BC. The era in which we observe other Illyrian kingdoms begins approximately at 400 BC and ends at 167 BC. The Autariatae under Pleurias (337 BC) were considered to have been a kingdom. The Kingdom of the <a href="/wiki/Ardiaei" title="Ardiaei">Ardiaei</a> (originally a tribe from the Neretva valley region) began at 230 BC and ended at 167 BC. The most notable Illyrian kingdoms and dynasties were those of Bardyllis of the Dardani and of Agron of the Ardiaei who created the last and best-known Illyrian kingdom. Agron ruled over the Ardiaei and had extended his rule to other tribes as well. </p>
      <p>From the 7th century BC, bronze was replaced by iron, after which only jewelry and art objects were still made out of bronze. Illyrian tribes, under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt" title="Hallstatt">Hallstatt</a> cultures to the north, formed regional centers that were slightly different. Parts of Central Bosnia were inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Daesitiates" title="Daesitiates">Daesitiates</a> tribe most commonly associated with <a href="/wiki/Central_Bosnian_cultural_group" title="Central Bosnian cultural group">Central Bosnian cultural group</a>. Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Glasinac_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasinac culture">Glasinac culture</a> is associated with <a href="/wiki/Autariatae" title="Autariatae">Autariatae</a> tribe. </p>
      <p>A very important role in their life was the cult of the dead, which is seen in their careful burials and burial ceremonies, as well as the richness of their burial sites. In northern parts, there was a long tradition of cremation and burial in shallow graves, while in the south the dead were buried in large stone or earth <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">tumuli</a> (natively called <i>gromile</i>) that in Herzegovina were reaching monumental sizes, more than 50 m wide and 5 m high. <i>Japodian tribes</i> had an affinity to decoration (heavy, oversized necklaces out of yellow, blue or white glass paste, and large bronze <a href="/wiki/Fibula_(brooch)" title="Fibula (brooch)">fibulas</a>, as well as spiral bracelets, diadems and helmets out of bronze foil). </p>
      <p>In the 4th century BC, the first invasion of <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> is recorded. They brought the technique of the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">pottery wheel</a>, new types of fibulas and different bronze and iron belts. They only passed on their way to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, so their influence in Bosnia and Herzegovina is negligible. Celtic migrations displaced many Illyrian tribes from their former lands, but some Celtic and Illyrian tribes mixed. Concrete historical evidence for this period is scarce, but overall it appears the region was populated by a number of different peoples speaking distinct languages. </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Neretva_Delta" title="Neretva Delta">Neretva Delta</a> in the south, there were important <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic</a> influence of the Illyrian <a href="/wiki/Daorson" title="Daorson">Daors</a> tribe. Their capital was <i>Daorson</i> in OÅ¡aniÄi near <a href="/wiki/Stolac" title="Stolac">Stolac</a>. Daorson in the 4th century BC was surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megalithic</a>, 5 m high stonewalls (as large as those of <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a> in Greece), composed of large trapezoid stone blocks. Daors made unique bronze coins and sculptures. </p>
      <p>Conflict between the Illyrians and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> started in 229&nbsp;BC, but Rome did not complete its annexation of the region until AD&nbsp;9. It was precisely in modern-day Bosnia and Herzegovina that Rome fought one of the most difficult battles in its history since the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>, as described by the Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> This was the Roman campaign against <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a>, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bellum_Batonianum" title="Bellum Batonianum">Bellum Batonianum</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> The conflict arose after an attempt to recruit Illyrians, and a revolt spanned for four years (6â9 AD), after which they were subdued.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> In the Roman period, Latin-speaking settlers from the entire <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> settled among the Illyrians, and Roman soldiers were encouraged to retire in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the split of the Empire between 337 and 395 AD, Dalmatia and Pannonia became parts of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. The region was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a> in 455 AD. It subsequently changed hands between the <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>. By the 6th century, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a> had reconquered the area for the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Slavs overwhelmed the Balkans in the 6th and 7th centuries. Illyrian cultural traits were adopted by the South Slavs, as evidenced in certain customs and traditions, placenames, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina in the Middle Ages">Bosnia and Herzegovina in the Middle Ages</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg/220px-Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="349" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg/330px-Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg/440px-Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="458" data-file-height="726"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hvalov_zbornik1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Hval%27s_Codex" title="Hval's Codex">Hval's Codex</a>, illustrated Slavic manuscript from medieval Bosnia.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Early Slavs</a> raided the Western Balkans, including Bosnia, in the 6th and early 7th century (amid the <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>), and were composed of small tribal units drawn from a single Slavic confederation known to the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Sclaveni" title="Sclaveni">Sclaveni</a></i> (whilst the related <i><a href="/wiki/Antes_(people)" title="Antes (people)">Antes</a></i>, roughly speaking, colonized the eastern portions of the Balkans).<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia_University_Press_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_University_Press-27">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Tribes recorded by the ethnonyms of "Serb" and "Croat" are described as a second, latter, migration of different people during the second quarter of the 7th century who do not seem to have been particularly numerous;<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia_University_Press_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_University_Press-27">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine199153_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine199153-29">[27]</a></sup> these early "Serb" and "Croat" tribes, whose exact identity is subject to scholarly debate,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> came to predominate over the Slavs in the neighbouring regions. The bulk of Bosnia proper, however, appears to have been a territory between Serb and Croat rule and is not enumerated as one of the regions settled by those tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine199153_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine199153-29">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bosnia is first mentioned <i>as a land (horion Bosona)</i> in Byzantine Emperor Constantine Porphyrogenitus' <i><a href="/wiki/De_Administrando_Imperio" title="De Administrando Imperio">De Administrando Imperio</a></i> in the mid 10th century, at the end of a chapter (Chap. 32) entitled <i>Of the Serbs and the country in which they now dwell</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-31">[29]</a></sup> This has been scholarly interpreted in several ways and used especially by the Serb national ideologists to prove Bosnia as originally a "Serb" land. Other scholars have asserted the inclusion of Bosnia into Chapter 32 to merely be the result of Serbian Grand Duke <a href="/wiki/%C4%8Caslav" title="Äaslav">Äaslav</a>'s temporary rule over Bosnia at the time, while also pointing out Porphyrogenitus does not say anywhere explicitly that Bosnia is a "Serb land".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-28_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-28-32">[30]</a></sup> In fact, the very translation of the critical sentence where the word <i>Bosona</i> (Bosnia) appears is subject to varying interpretation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-31">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In time, Bosnia formed a unit under its own ruler, who called himself Bosnian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine199153_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine199153-29">[27]</a></sup> Bosnia, along with other territories, became part of <a href="/wiki/Duklja" title="Duklja">Duklja</a> in the 11th century, although it retained its own nobility and institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1991223_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1991223-33">[31]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg/220px-Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg/330px-Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg/440px-Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="941" data-file-height="709"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bosnia in the Middle Ages spanning the <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Banate of Bosnia</a> and the succeeding <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bosnia" title="Kingdom of Bosnia">Kingdom of Bosnia</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> political circumstance led to the area being contested between the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Following another shift of power between the two in the early 12th century, Bosnia found itself outside the control of both and emerged as the <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Banate of Bosnia</a> (under the rule of local <i><a href="/wiki/Ban_(title)" title="Ban (title)">bans</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> The first Bosnian ban known by name was <a href="/wiki/Ban_Bori%C4%87" title="Ban BoriÄ">Ban BoriÄ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1991288_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1991288-35">[33]</a></sup> The second was <a href="/wiki/Ban_Kulin" title="Ban Kulin">Ban Kulin</a> whose rule marked the start of a controversy involving the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_Church" title="Bosnian Church">Bosnian Church</a> â considered heretical by the Roman Catholic Church. In response to Hungarian attempts to use church politics regarding the issue as a way to reclaim sovereignty over Bosnia, Kulin held a council of local church leaders to renounce the heresy and embraced Catholicism in 1203. Despite this, Hungarian ambitions remained unchanged long after Kulin's death in 1204, waning only after an unsuccessful invasion in 1254. During this time the population was called <i>Dobri <a href="/wiki/Bo%C5%A1njani" title="BoÅ¡njani">BoÅ¡njani</a></i> ("Good Bosnians").<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> The names Serb and Croat, though occasionally appearing in peripheral areas, were not used in Bosnia proper.<sup id="cite_ref-lrb.co.uk_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lrb.co.uk-38">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bosnian history from then until the early 14th century was marked by a power struggle between the <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0ubi%C4%87_family" title="Å ubiÄ family">Å ubiÄ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kotromani%C4%87_dynasty" title="KotromaniÄ dynasty">KotromaniÄ</a> families. This conflict came to an end in 1322, when <a href="/wiki/Stephen_II,_Ban_of_Bosnia" title="Stephen II, Ban of Bosnia">Stephen II KotromaniÄ</a> became <i>Ban</i>. By the time of his death in 1353, he was successful in annexing territories to the north and west, as well as Zahumlje and parts of Dalmatia. He was succeeded by his ambitious nephew <a href="/wiki/Tvrtko_I_of_Bosnia" title="Tvrtko I of Bosnia">Tvrtko</a> who, following a prolonged struggle with nobility and inter-family strife, gained full control of the country in 1367. By the year 1377, Bosnia was elevated into a kingdom with the coronation of Tvrtko as the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Bosnia" title="List of rulers of Bosnia">Bosnian King</a> in Mile near <a href="/wiki/Visoko_during_the_Middle_Ages" title="Visoko during the Middle Ages">Visoko</a> in the Bosnian heartland.<sup id="cite_ref-Mile_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mile-39">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-An_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An-40">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following his death in 1391 however, Bosnia fell into a long period of decline. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> had started its <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">conquest of Europe</a> and posed a major threat to the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> throughout the first half of the 15th century. Finally, after decades of political and social instability, the Kingdom of Bosnia ceased to exist in 1463 after its conquest by the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Empire">Ottoman Empire</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_conquest_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Ottoman conquest of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Ottoman conquest of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Ottoman Bosnia and Herzegovina">Ottoman Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg/200px-Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg/300px-Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg/400px-Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="5184"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque_IMG_9523_sarajevo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Gazi_Husrev-beg_Mosque" title="Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque">Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> dating from 1531
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Ottoman conquest of Bosnia marked a new era in the country's history and introduced drastic changes in the political and cultural landscape. The Ottomans incorporating Bosnia as an integral province of the Ottoman Empire with its historical name and territorial integrity.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Within Bosnia the Ottomans introduced a number of key changes in the territory's socio-political administration; including a new landholding system, a reorganization of administrative units, and a complex system of social differentiation by class and religious affiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The four centuries of Ottoman rule also had a drastic impact on Bosnia's population make-up, which changed several times as a result of the empire's conquests, frequent wars with European powers, forced and economic migrations, and epidemics. A native Slavic-speaking Muslim community emerged and eventually became the largest of the ethno-religious groups due to lack of strong Christian church organizations and continuous rivalry between the Orthodox and Catholic churches, while the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_Church" title="Bosnian Church">Bosnian Church</a> disappeared altogether (ostensibly by conversion of its members to Islam). The Ottomans referred to them as <i>kristianlar</i> while the Orthodox and Catholics were called <i>gebir</i> or <i>kafir</i>, meaning "unbeliever".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja200329â30_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja200329â30-44">[42]</a></sup> The Bosnian <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a> (and the Catholic population as a whole) were protected by official imperial decrees and in accordance and full extent of Ottoman laws, however in effect, these often merely affected arbitrary rule and behavior of powerful local elite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As the Ottoman Empire continued their rule in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">Rumelia</a>), Bosnia was somewhat relieved of the pressures of being a frontier province, and experienced a period of general welfare. A number of cities, such as Sarajevo and <a href="/wiki/Mostar" title="Mostar">Mostar</a>, were established and grew into regional centers of trade and urban culture and were then visited by <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> traveler <a href="/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi" title="Evliya Ãelebi">Evliya Ãelebi</a> in 1648. Within these cities, various Ottoman Sultans financed the construction of many works of <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Architecture of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnian architecture</a> such as the country's first library in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrassas</a>, a school of <a href="/wiki/Sufi_philosophy" title="Sufi philosophy">Sufi philosophy</a>, and a clock tower (<i>Sahat Kula</i>), bridges such as the <a href="/wiki/Stari_Most" title="Stari Most">Stari Most</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Emperor%27s_Mosque" title="Emperor's Mosque">Emperor's Mosque</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gazi_Husrev-beg_Mosque" title="Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque">Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Furthermore, several Bosnian Muslims played influential roles in the Ottoman Empire's cultural and <a href="/wiki/Political_history" title="Political history">political history</a> during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-45">[43]</a></sup> Bosnian recruits formed a large component of the Ottoman ranks in the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Moh%C3%A1cs" title="Battle of MohÃ¡cs">MohÃ¡cs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Krbava_Field" title="Battle of Krbava Field">Krbava field</a>, while numerous other Bosnians rose through the ranks of the Ottoman military to occupy the highest positions of power in the Empire, including admirals such as <a href="/wiki/Matrak%C3%A7%C4%B1_Nasuh" title="MatrakÃ§Ä± Nasuh">MatrakÃ§Ä± Nasuh</a>; generals such as <a href="/wiki/Isa-Beg_Ishakovi%C4%87" title="Isa-Beg IshakoviÄ">Isa-Beg IshakoviÄ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gazi_Husrev-beg" title="Gazi Husrev-beg">Gazi Husrev-beg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telli_Hasan_Pasha" title="Telli Hasan Pasha">Telli Hasan Pasha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sar%C4%B1_S%C3%BCleyman_Pasha" title="SarÄ± SÃ¼leyman Pasha">SarÄ± SÃ¼leyman Pasha</a>; administrators such as <a href="/wiki/Ferhad_Pasha_Sokolovi%C4%87" title="Ferhad Pasha SokoloviÄ">Ferhad Pasha SokoloviÄ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osman_Grada%C5%A1%C4%8Devi%C4%87" title="Osman GradaÅ¡ÄeviÄ">Osman GradaÅ¡ÄeviÄ</a>; and Grand <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">Viziers</a> such as the influential <a href="/wiki/Sokollu_Mehmed_Pasha" title="Sokollu Mehmed Pasha">Sokollu Mehmed Pasha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damat_Ibrahim_Pasha" title="Damat Ibrahim Pasha">Damat Ibrahim Pasha</a>. Some Bosnians emerged as <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> mystics, scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Muhamed_Hevaji_Uskufi_Bosnevi" title="Muhamed Hevaji Uskufi Bosnevi">Muhamed Hevaji Uskufi Bosnevi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_D%C5%BEabi%C4%87" title="Ali DÅ¾abiÄ">Ali DÅ¾abiÄ</a>; and poets in the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ImamoviÄ,_Mustafa_1996_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImamoviÄ,_Mustafa_1996-46">[44]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo_1878..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sarajevo_1878..jpg/220px-Sarajevo_1878..jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sarajevo_1878..jpg/330px-Sarajevo_1878..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sarajevo_1878..jpg/440px-Sarajevo_1878..jpg 2x" data-file-width="716" data-file-height="518"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo_1878..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Austro-Hungarian troops <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_campaign_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_in_1878" title="Austro-Hungarian campaign in Bosnia and Herzegovina in 1878">enter Sarajevo</a>, 1878
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>However, by the late 17th century the Empire's military misfortunes caught up with the country, and the end of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Turkish_War" title="Great Turkish War">Great Turkish War</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Karlowitz" title="Treaty of Karlowitz">treaty of Karlowitz</a> in 1699 again made Bosnia the Empire's westernmost province. The 18th century was marked by further military failures, numerous revolts within Bosnia, and several outbursts of plague.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Porte's efforts at modernizing the Ottoman state were met with distrust growing to hostility in Bosnia, where local aristocrats stood to lose much through the proposed <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a> reforms. This, combined with frustrations over territorial, political concessions in the north-east, and the plight of <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> refugees arriving from the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Smederevo" title="Sanjak of Smederevo">Sanjak of Smederevo</a> into <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_Eyalet" title="Bosnia Eyalet">Bosnia Eyalet</a>, culminated in a partially unsuccessful revolt by <a href="/wiki/Husein_Grada%C5%A1%C4%8Devi%C4%87" title="Husein GradaÅ¡ÄeviÄ">Husein GradaÅ¡ÄeviÄ</a>, who endorsed a <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_Eyalet" title="Bosnia Eyalet">Bosnia Eyalet</a> autonomous from the authoritarian rule of the Ottoman Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a>, who persecuted, executed and abolished the <a href="/wiki/Janissary" title="Janissary">Janissaries</a> and reduced the role of autonomous <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pashas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">Rumelia</a>. Mahmud II sent his <a href="/wiki/Grand_vizier" title="Grand vizier">Grand vizier</a> to subdue Bosnia Eyalet and succeeded only with the reluctant assistance of <a href="/wiki/Ali_Pasha_Rizvanbegovi%C4%87" title="Ali Pasha RizvanbegoviÄ">Ali Pasha RizvanbegoviÄ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ImamoviÄ,_Mustafa_1996_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImamoviÄ,_Mustafa_1996-46">[44]</a></sup> Related rebellions were extinguished by 1850, but the situation continued to deteriorate. </p>
      <p>New nationalist movements appeared in Bosnia by the middle of the 19th century. Bolstered by Serbia's breakaway from the Ottoman Empire in the early 19th century, Serbian nationalists began making contacts and sending nationalist propaganda claiming Bosnia as a Serbian province. In the neighboring Habsburg Empire across the Ottoman border, Croatian nationalists made similar claims about Bosnia as a Croatian province. The rise of these competing movements marked the beginning of nationalist politics in Bosnia, which continued to grow in the rest of the 19th and 20th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Agrarian unrest eventually sparked the <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina_uprising_(1875%E2%80%931877)" title="Herzegovina uprising (1875â1877)">Herzegovinian rebellion</a>, a widespread peasant uprising, in 1875. The conflict rapidly spread and came to involve several Balkan states and Great Powers, a situation that led to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Treaty of Berlin</a> in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Austro-Hungarian_Empire">Austro-Hungarian Empire</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_rule_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Austro-Hungarian rule in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Austro-Hungarian rule in Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>
      </div> 
      <p>At the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> in 1878, the <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a> Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Gyula_Andr%C3%A1ssy" title="Gyula AndrÃ¡ssy">Gyula AndrÃ¡ssy</a> obtained the occupation and administration of Bosnia and Herzegovina, and he also obtained the right to station garrisons in the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Novi_Pazar" title="Sanjak of Novi Pazar">Sanjak of Novi Pazar</a>, which would remain under <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> administration until 1908, when the Austro-Hungarian troops withdrew from the Sanjak. </p>
      <p>Although Austro-Hungarian officials quickly came to an agreement with Bosnians, tensions remained and a mass emigration of Bosnians occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> However, a state of relative stability was reached soon enough and Austro-Hungarian authorities were able to embark on a number of social and administrative reforms they intended would make Bosnia and Herzegovina into a "model" colony. </p>
      <p>Habsburg rule had several key concerns in Bosnia. It tried to dissipate the South Slav nationalism by disputing the earlier Serb and Croat claims to Bosnia and encouraging identification of Bosnian or <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniak</a> identity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHajdarpasic2015161-165_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHajdarpasic2015161-165-49">[47]</a></sup> Habsburg rule also tried to provide for modernisation by codifying laws, introducing new political institutions, and establishing and expanding industries.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>AustriaâHungary began to plan annexation of Bosnia, but due to international disputes the issue was not resolved until the annexation crisis of 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini200594_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini200594-51">[49]</a></sup> Several external matters affected status of Bosnia and its relationship with AustriaâHungary. <a href="/wiki/May_Coup_(Serbia)" title="May Coup (Serbia)">A bloody coup</a> occurred in Serbia in 1903, which brought a radical anti-Austrian government into power in Belgrade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005140_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005140-52">[50]</a></sup> Then in 1908, the revolt in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> raised concerns the Istanbul government might seek the outright return of Bosnia-Herzegovina. These factors caused the Austro-Hungarian government to seek a permanent resolution of the Bosnian question sooner, rather than later. </p>
      <p>Taking advantage of turmoil in the Ottoman Empire, Austro-Hungarian diplomacy tried to obtain provisional Russian approval for changes over the status of Bosnia Herzegovina and published the annexation proclamation on 6 October 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005227_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005227-53">[51]</a></sup> Despite international objections to the Austro-Hungarian annexation, Russians and their client state, Serbia, were compelled to accept the Austrian-Hungarian annexation of Bosnia Herzegovina in March 1909. </p>
      <p>In 1910, Habsburg Emperor Franz Joseph proclaimed the first constitution in Bosnia, which led to relaxation of earlier laws, elections and formation of the Bosnian parliament, and growth of new political life.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg/220px-DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg/330px-DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg/440px-DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1326" data-file-height="1606"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Assassination of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sophie,_Duchess_of_Hohenberg" title="Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg">Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg</a> in Sarajevo by <a href="/wiki/Gavrilo_Princip" title="Gavrilo Princip">Gavrilo Princip</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 28 June 1914, a Yugoslav nationalist youth named <a href="/wiki/Gavrilo_Princip" title="Gavrilo Princip">Gavrilo Princip</a>, a member of the secret Serbian-supported movement, <a href="/wiki/Young_Bosnia" title="Young Bosnia">Young Bosnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand" title="Assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand">assassinated</a> the heir to the Austro-Hungarian throne, <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">Archduke Franz Ferdinand</a>, in Sarajevoâan event that was the spark that set off World War I. At the end of the war, the <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniaks</a> had lost more men per capita than any other ethnic group in the Habsburg Empire whilst serving in the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian-Herzegovinian_Infantry" title="Bosnian-Herzegovinian Infantry">Bosnian-Herzegovinian Infantry</a> (known as <i>Bosniaken</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Army" title="Austro-Hungarian Army">Austro-Hungarian Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schachinger_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schachinger-55">[53]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Bosnia and Herzegovina as a whole managed to escape the conflict relatively unscathed.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-45">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Austro-Hungarian authorities established an auxiliary militia known as the <a href="/wiki/Schutzkorps" title="Schutzkorps">Schutzkorps</a> with a moot role in the empire's policy of <a href="/wiki/Anti-Serb_sentiment" title="Anti-Serb sentiment">anti-Serb</a> repression.<sup id="cite_ref-Banac1988_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banac1988-56">[54]</a></sup> Schutzkorps, predominantly recruited among the Muslim (Bosniak) population, were tasked with hunting down rebel Serbs (the <i><a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Komitadji" title="Komitadji">Komitadji</a></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141-57">[55]</a></sup> and became known for their persecution of <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> particularly in Serb populated areas of eastern Bosnia, where they partly retaliated against Serbian <a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a> who in fall 1914 had carried out attacks against the Muslim population in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> The proceedings of the Austro-Hungarian authorities led to around 5,500 citizens of Serb ethnicity in Bosnia and Herzegovina being arrested, and between 700 and 2,200 died in prison while 460 were executed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141-57">[55]</a></sup> Around 5,200 Serb families were forcibly expelled from Bosnia and Herzegovina.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141-57">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia">Kingdom of Yugoslavia</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina#Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia_(1918â41)" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina Â§&nbsp;Kingdom of Yugoslavia (1918â41)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Following World War I, Bosnia and Herzegovina joined the South Slav <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">Kingdom of Serbs, Croats and Slovenes</a> (soon renamed Yugoslavia). Political life in Bosnia at this time was marked by two major trends: social and economic unrest over <a href="/wiki/Distribution_(economics)" title="Distribution (economics)">property redistribution</a>, and formation of several political parties that frequently changed coalitions and alliances with parties in other Yugoslav regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-45">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The dominant ideological conflict of the Yugoslav state, between Croatian regionalism and Serbian centralization, was approached differently by Bosnia's major <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a> and was dependent on the overall political atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> The political reforms brought about in the newly established Yugoslavian kingdom saw few benefits for the Bosniaks; according to the 1910 final census of land ownership and population according to religious affiliation conducted in Austro-Hungary, Muslims (Bosniaks) owned 91.1%, Orthodox Serbians owned 6.0%, Croatian Catholics owned 2.6% and others, 0.3% of the property. Following the reforms Bosnian Muslims were dispossessed of a total of 1,175,305 hectares of agricultural and forest land.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Although the initial split of the country into 33 <a href="/wiki/Oblast" title="Oblast">oblasts</a> erased the presence of traditional geographic entities from the map, the efforts of Bosnian politicians such as <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_Spaho" title="Mehmed Spaho">Mehmed Spaho</a> ensured the six oblasts carved up from Bosnia and Herzegovina corresponded to the six sanjaks from Ottoman times and, thus, matched the country's traditional boundary as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The establishment of the Kingdom of Yugoslavia in 1929, however, brought the redrawing of administrative regions into <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia#Subdivisions" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">banates</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Banovina_(region)" title="Banovina (region)">banovinas</a></i> that purposely avoided all historical and ethnic lines, removing any trace of a Bosnian entity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> Serbo-Croat tensions over the structuring of the Yugoslav state continued, with the concept of a separate Bosnian division receiving little or no consideration. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Cvetkovi%C4%87%E2%80%93Ma%C4%8Dek_Agreement" title="CvetkoviÄâMaÄek Agreement">CvetkoviÄ-MaÄek Agreement</a> that created the <a href="/wiki/Banovina_of_Croatia" title="Banovina of Croatia">Croatian banate</a> in 1939 encouraged what was essentially a <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Partition of Bosnia and Herzegovina">partition of Bosnia</a> between Croatia and Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-ImamoviÄ,_Mustafa_1996_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImamoviÄ,_Mustafa_1996-46">[44]</a></sup> However the rising threat of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> forced Yugoslav politicians to shift their attention. Following a period that saw attempts at appeasement, the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Treaty</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Yugoslav coup d'Ã©tat">coup d'Ã©tat</a>, Yugoslavia was finally invaded by Germany on 6 April 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="World_War_II_.281941.E2.80.9345.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_(1941â45)">World War II (1941â45)</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1941%E2%80%9345)" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1941â45)">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1941â45)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Neretva_most.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Neretva_most.jpg/220px-Neretva_most.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Neretva_most.jpg/330px-Neretva_most.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Neretva_most.jpg/440px-Neretva_most.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="685"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Neretva_most.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The railway bridge over the <a href="/wiki/Neretva" title="Neretva">Neretva</a> river in <a href="/wiki/Jablanica,_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Jablanica, Bosnia and Herzegovina">Jablanica</a>, twice destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Case_White" title="Case White">Case White</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Once the kingdom of Yugoslavia was conquered by German forces in World War II, all of Bosnia was ceded to the Nazi puppet regime, the <a href="/wiki/Independent_State_of_Croatia" title="Independent State of Croatia">Independent State of Croatia</a> (NDH) led by the <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" title="UstaÅ¡e">UstaÅ¡e</a>. The NDH leaders embarked on a <a href="/wiki/Genocide_of_Serbs_in_the_Independent_State_of_Croatia" title="Genocide of Serbs in the Independent State of Croatia">campaign of extermination</a> of Serbs, Jews, <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a> as well as dissident Croats, and, later, <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Partisans" title="Yugoslav Partisans">Partisans</a> by setting up a number of <a href="/wiki/Jasenovac_concentration_camp" title="Jasenovac concentration camp">death camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> The regime systematically and brutally massacred Serbs in villages in the countryside, using a variety of tools.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> The scale of the violence meant that approximately every sixth Serb living in Bosnia-Herzegovina was the victim of a massacre and virtually every Serb had a family member that was killed in the war, mostly by the UstaÅ¡e. The experience had a profound impact in the collective memory of Serbs in Croatia and Bosnia.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> An estimated 209,000 Serbs or 16.9% of its Bosnia population were killed on the territory of BosniaâHerzegovina during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" title="UstaÅ¡e">UstaÅ¡e</a> recognized both Roman Catholicism and Islam as the national religions, but held the position <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, as a symbol of Serbian identity, was their greatest foe.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramet,_p._118_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramet,_p._118-65">[63]</a></sup> Although Croats were by far the largest ethnic group to constitute the UstaÅ¡e, the Vice President of the NDH and leader of the Yugoslav Muslim Organization <a href="/wiki/D%C5%BEafer_Kulenovi%C4%87" title="DÅ¾afer KulenoviÄ">DÅ¾afer KulenoviÄ</a> was a Muslim, and Muslims (Bosniaks) in total constituted nearly 12% of the UstaÅ¡e military and civil service authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003179_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003179-66">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many Serbs themselves took up arms and joined the <a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a>, a Serb nationalist movement with the aim of establishing an ethnically homogeneous '<a href="/wiki/Greater_Serbia" title="Greater Serbia">Greater Serbian</a>' state<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> within the Kingdom of Yugoslavia. The Chetniks, in turn, persecuted and killed a large number of non-Serbs, communist Serbs and Communist sympathizers, with the Muslim population of Bosnia, Herzegovina and <a href="/wiki/Sand%C5%BEak" title="SandÅ¾ak">SandÅ¾ak</a> being a primary target.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> Once captured, Muslim villagers were systematically massacred by the Chetniks.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> Of the 75,000 Muslims who lost their lives in Bosnia and Herzegovina during the war,<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-70">[68]</a></sup> approximately 30,000 (mostly civilians) were killed by the Chetniks.<sup id="cite_ref-Geiger_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geiger-71">[69]</a></sup> Between 64,000 and 79,000 Bosnian Croats were killed between April 1941 to May 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-70">[68]</a></sup> Of these, about 18,000 were killed by the Chetniks.<sup id="cite_ref-Geiger_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geiger-71">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra,_Sarajevo_(by_Pudelek).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra%2C_Sarajevo_%28by_Pudelek%29.JPG/220px-Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra%2C_Sarajevo_%28by_Pudelek%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra%2C_Sarajevo_%28by_Pudelek%29.JPG/330px-Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra%2C_Sarajevo_%28by_Pudelek%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra%2C_Sarajevo_%28by_Pudelek%29.JPG/440px-Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra%2C_Sarajevo_%28by_Pudelek%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1810" data-file-height="2315"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Vje%C4%8Dna_vatra,_Sarajevo_(by_Pudelek).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Eternal_flame_(Sarajevo)" title="Eternal flame (Sarajevo)">Eternal flame</a> memorial to the military and civilian World War II victims.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A percentage of Muslims served in Nazi <a href="/wiki/13th_Waffen_Mountain_Division_of_the_SS_Handschar_(1st_Croatian)" title="13th Waffen Mountain Division of the SS Handschar (1st Croatian)"><i>Waffen-SS</i></a> units.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> These units were responsible for massacres of Serbs in northwest and eastern Bosnia, most notably in <a href="/wiki/Vlasenica" title="Vlasenica">Vlasenica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> On 12 October 1941, a group of 108 prominent Sarajevan Muslims signed the <a href="/wiki/Resolution_of_Sarajevo_Muslims" title="Resolution of Sarajevo Muslims">Resolution of Sarajevo Muslims</a> by which they condemned the persecution of Serbs organized by the <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" title="UstaÅ¡e">UstaÅ¡e</a>, made distinction between Muslims who participated in such persecutions and the Muslim population as a whole, presented information about the persecutions of Muslims by Serbs, and requested security for all citizens of the country, regardless of their identity.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Starting in 1941, Yugoslav communists under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a> organized their own multi-ethnic resistance group, the <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Partisans" title="Yugoslav Partisans">partisans</a>, who fought against both Axis and Chetnik forces. On 29 November 1943 the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Fascist_Council_for_the_National_Liberation_of_Yugoslavia" title="Anti-Fascist Council for the National Liberation of Yugoslavia">Anti-Fascist Council for the National Liberation of Yugoslavia</a> with Tito at its helm held a founding conference in <a href="/wiki/Jajce" title="Jajce">Jajce</a> where Bosnia and Herzegovina was reestablished as a republic within the Yugoslavian federation in its Habsburg borders.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> During the entire course of the WWII in Yugoslavia, 64.1% of all Bosnian Partisans were Serbs, 23% were Muslims and 8.8% Croats.<sup id="cite_ref-anubih.ba-Hoare_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anubih.ba-Hoare-76">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Military success eventually prompted the Allies to support the Partisans, resulting in the successful <a href="/wiki/Maclean_Mission" title="Maclean Mission">Maclean Mission</a>, but Tito declined their offer to help and relied on his own forces instead. All the major military offensives by the antifascist movement of Yugoslavia against Nazis and their local supporters were conducted in BosniaâHerzegovina and its peoples bore the brunt of fighting. More than 300,000 people died in Bosnia and Herzegovina in World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> At the end of the war the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/1946_Yugoslav_Constitution" title="1946 Yugoslav Constitution">constitution of 1946</a>, officially made Bosnia and Herzegovina one of six constituent republics in the new state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Socialist_Federative_Republic_of_Yugoslavia_.281945.E2.80.931992.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_Federative_Republic_of_Yugoslavia_(1945â1992)">Socialist Federative Republic of Yugoslavia (1945â1992)</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Socialist Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1945â1992)</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Socialist Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Socialist Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1946%E2%80%931992).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_%281946%E2%80%931992%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1946%E2%80%931992).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bosnia and Herzegovina's flag while in the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Due to its central geographic position within the Yugoslavian federation, post-war Bosnia was selected as a base for the development of the military defense industry. This contributed to a large concentration of arms and military personnel in Bosnia; a significant factor in <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">the war that followed the break-up of Yugoslavia</a> in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[17]</a></sup> However, Bosnia's existence within Yugoslavia, for the large part, was relatively peaceful and very prosperous, with high employment, a strong industrial and export oriented economy, a good education system and social and medical security for every citizen (of Bosnia and Herzegovina). Several international corporations operated in Bosnia â <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> as part of TAS (car factory in Sarajevo, from 1972), <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> (from 1975), SKF Sweden (from 1967), <a href="/wiki/Marlboro_(cigarette)" title="Marlboro (cigarette)">Marlboro</a>, (a tobacco factory in Sarajevo), and <a href="/wiki/Holiday_Inn" title="Holiday Inn">Holiday Inn</a> hotels. Sarajevo was the site of the <a href="/wiki/1984_Winter_Olympics" title="1984 Winter Olympics">1984 Winter Olympics</a>. </p>
      <p>During the 1950s and 1960s Bosnia was a political backwater of the Republic of Yugoslavia. In the 1970s a strong Bosnian political elite arose, fueled in part by Tito's leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and Bosnians serving in Yugoslavia's diplomatic corps. While working within the Socialist system, politicians such as <a href="/wiki/D%C5%BEemal_Bijedi%C4%87" title="DÅ¾emal BijediÄ">DÅ¾emal BijediÄ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Branko_Mikuli%C4%87" title="Branko MikuliÄ">Branko MikuliÄ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamdija_Pozderac" title="Hamdija Pozderac">Hamdija Pozderac</a> reinforced and protected the sovereignty of Bosnia and Herzegovina.<sup id="cite_ref-Stojic_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stojic-78">[76]</a></sup> Their efforts proved key during the turbulent period following Tito's death in 1980, and are today considered some of the early steps towards Bosnian independence. However, the republic did not escape the increasingly nationalistic climate of the time. With the fall of communism and the start of the break-up of Yugoslavia, doctrine of tolerance began to lose its potency, creating an opportunity for nationalist elements in the society to spread their influence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Bosnian_War_.281992.E2.80.931995.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bosnian_War_(1992â1995)">Bosnian War (1992â1995)</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif/320px-Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif" decoding="async" width="320" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif/480px-Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif/640px-Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif 2x" data-file-width="1545" data-file-height="1242"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Dissolution process of <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 18 November 1990, multi-party parliamentary elections were held throughout Bosnia and Herzegovina. A second round followed on 25 November, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly" title="National Assembly">national assembly</a> where communist power was replaced by a coalition of three ethnically-based parties.<sup id="cite_ref-phron1_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phron1-79">[77]</a></sup> Following <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>'s declarations of independence from Yugoslavia, a significant split developed among the residents of Bosnia and Herzegovina on the issue of whether to remain within Yugoslavia (overwhelmingly favored by Serbs) or seek independence (overwhelmingly favored by Bosniaks and Croats).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The Serb members of parliament, consisting mainly of the <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Democratic_Party_(Bosnia_and_Herzegovina)" class="mw-redirect" title="Serbian Democratic Party (Bosnia and Herzegovina)">Serb Democratic Party</a> members, abandoned the central parliament in Sarajevo, and formed the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_the_Republika_Srpska" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of the Republika Srpska">Assembly of the Serb People of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> on 24 October 1991, which marked the end of the tri-ethnic coalition that governed after the elections in 1990. This Assembly established the Serbian Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina in part of the territory of Bosnia and Herzegovina on 9 January 1992. It was renamed <a href="/wiki/Republika_Srpska_(1992%E2%80%9395)" class="mw-redirect" title="Republika Srpska (1992â95)">Republika Srpska</a> in August 1992. On 18 November 1991, the party branch in Bosnia and Herzegovina of the ruling party in the Republic of Croatia, the <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Democratic_Union" title="Croatian Democratic Union">Croatian Democratic Union</a> (HDZ), proclaimed the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Republic_of_Herzeg-Bosnia" title="Croatian Republic of Herzeg-Bosnia">Croatian Community of Herzeg-Bosnia</a> in a separate part of the territory of Bosnia and Herzegovina with the <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Defence_Council" title="Croatian Defence Council">Croatian Defence Council</a> (HVO) as its military branch.<sup id="cite_ref-ICTY:_PrliÄ_et_al._(IT-04-74)_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICTY:_PrliÄ_et_al._(IT-04-74)-80">[78]</a></sup> It went unrecognized by the Government of Bosnia and Herzegovina, which declared it illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg/220px-Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg/330px-Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="506"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Parliament of Bosnia and Herzegovina burns after being struck by tank fire during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sarajevo" title="Siege of Sarajevo">Siege of Sarajevo</a>, 1992
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A declaration of the sovereignty of Bosnia and Herzegovina on 15 October 1991 was followed by a referendum for independence on 29 February/1 March 1992, which was boycotted by the great majority of Serbs. The turnout in the independence referendum was 63.4 percent and 99.7 percent of voters voted for independence.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> Bosnia and Herzegovina declared independence on 3 March 1992 and received international recognition the following month on 6 April 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> was admitted as a member state of the United Nations on 22 May 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Serbian leader <a href="/wiki/Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87" title="Slobodan MiloÅ¡eviÄ">Slobodan MiloÅ¡eviÄ</a> and Croatian leader <a href="/wiki/Franjo_Tu%C4%91man" title="Franjo TuÄman">Franjo TuÄman</a> are believed to have agreed on a <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Partition of Bosnia and Herzegovina">partition of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> in March 1991, with the aim of establishing <a href="/wiki/Greater_Serbia" title="Greater Serbia">Greater Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greater_Croatia" title="Greater Croatia">Greater Croatia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following Bosnia and Herzegovina's declaration of independence, Bosnian Serb militias mobilized in different parts of the country.
